\[(x^6 - 1)(x^6 + 1) = (x^3 - 1)(x^3 + 1)(x^2 + 1)(x^4 - x^2 + 1).\]Note that $x^3 - 1$ factors as
\[x^3 - 1 = (x - 1)(x^2 + x + 1),\]and $x^3 + 1$ factors as
\[x^3 + 1 = (x + 1)(x^2 - x + 1).\]Also, we can write $x^4 - x^2 + 1$ as $\frac{x^6 + 1}{x^2 + 1}.$  Then
\[x^{12} - 1 = (x - 1)(x + 1)(x^2 + 1)(x^2 + x + 1)(x^2 - x + 1) \cdot \frac{x^6 + 1}{x^2 + 1}.\]The quadratic factors $x^2 + 1,$ $x^2 + x + 1,$ and $x^2 - x + 1$ cannot be factored further over the integers, and $\frac{x^6 + 1}{x^2 + 1}$ also cannot be factored further over the integers, as long as we don't expand in terms of $x.$

Thus, $k = \boxed{5}.$

We want to write $G_n$ in terms of $G_{n-1}$ and $G_{n-2}$.  Since $G_n = F_{3n}$, this is the same as writing $F_{3n}$ in terms of $F_{3(n-1)}$ and $F_{3(n-2)}$.  To do this, we repeatedly apply the recurrence relation given to us.

$$ \begin{aligned} G_n &= F_{3n} \\
&=F_{3n-1} + F_{3n-2} \\
&=2F_{3n-2} + F_{3n-3} \\
&=3F_{3n-3} + 2F_{3n-4} \\
&=3F_{3n-3} + F_{3n-4} +F_{3n-5} + F_{3n-6} \\
&=4F_{3n-3} + F_{3n-6} \\
&=4G_{n-1} + G_{n-2}.
\end{aligned}$$Hence, $(a,b) = \boxed{(4,1)}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We have that
\begin{align*}
G_2 &= F_6 = 8, \\
G_3 &= F_9 = 34, \\
G_4 &= F_{12} = 144, \\
G_5 &= F_{15} = 610.
\end{align*}We want to find constants $a$ and $b$ such that $G_n = a G_{n - 1} + b G_{n - 2}$ for every integer $n \ge 3.$

Multiplying the recursion $F_n = F_{n - 1} + F_{n - 2}$ by $F_{n - 3},$ we get
\[F_{n - 3} F_n = F_{n - 3} F_{n - 1} + F_{n - 3} F_{n - 2}.\]Summing over all $n \ge 4,$ we get
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n = 4}^\infty F_{n - 3} F_n &= \sum_{n = 4}^\infty F_{n - 3} F_{n - 1} + \sum_{n = 4}^\infty F_{n - 3} F_{n - 2} \\
&= \sum_{n = 1}^\infty F_{n + 2} F_n + \sum_{n = 2}^\infty F_{n + 1} F_{n - 1} \\
&= \sum_{n = 1}^\infty F_n^2 + \sum_{n = 1}^\infty F_n^2 \\
&= 2 \sum_{n = 1}^\infty F_n^2.
\end{align*}Also,
\[\sum_{n = 4}^\infty F_{n - 3} F_n = F_1 F_4 + F_2 F_5 + F_3 F_6 + F_4 F_7 + F_5 F_8 + \dotsb = F_1 G_2 + F_2 G_3 + F_3 G_4 + F_4 G_5 + \dotsb.\]Hence,
\[G_2 F_1 + G_3 F_2 + G_4 F_3 + G_5 F_4 + \dotsb = 2 \sum_{n = 1}^\infty F_n^2.\]Since $G_n = F_{3n}$ for every integer $n,$
\[G_2 F_1 + G_3 F_2 + G_4 F_3 + G_5 F_4 + \dotsb = 2 \sum_{n = 1}^\infty F_{3n}^2.\]Then $8 \cdot 1 + 34 \cdot 1 + 144 \cdot 2 + 610 \cdot 3 + \dotsb = 2 \cdot \left( 0^2 + F_3^2 + F_6^2 + F_9^2 + F_{12}^2 + \dotsb \right),$ or
\[8 + 34 + 288 + 1830 + \dotsb = 2 \cdot \left( 0^2 + 1^2 + 2^2 + 5^2 + \dotsb \right).\]Thus,
\[4 + 17 + 144 + 915 + \dotsb = 2 \cdot \left( 0 + 1 + 4 + 25 + \dotsb \right).\]Matching coefficients, we get
\begin{align*}
a &= \frac{144}{34}, \\
b &= \frac{-915}{34}.
\end{align*}Hence, $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( \frac{72}{17}, -\frac{915}{34} \right)}.$

$$5x^2- 17x + 6 = 0.$$Factoring gives us
$$(x-3)(5x-2) = 0.$$Hence, $x$ must be $\boxed{3}$ or $\boxed{\frac{2}{5}}$.

To begin solving this problem, let's first get a common denominator on both sides of the equation. We have \begin{align*}
\frac{2x^2-3x}{x^2-x} + 5x -11 &= \frac{3x^2+5x+2}{x^2-1} \\
\frac{2x^2-3x}{x(x-1)} + 5x -11 &= \frac{(x+2)(3x+1)}{(x-1)(x+1)} \\
\frac{2x^2-3x}{x(x-1)} + \frac{-5(x-1)x}{x(x-1)} + \frac{11x(x-1)}{x(x-1)} &= \frac{(x+2)(3x+1)}{(x-1)(x+1)} \\
\frac{2x^2-3x-5x^2+5x+11x^2-11x}{x(x-1)} &= \frac{(x+2)(3x+1)}{(x-1)(x+1)} \\
\frac{8x^2-9x}{x(x-1)} &= \frac{(x+2)(3x+1)}{(x-1)(x+1)}.
\end{align*} Next, we can cross-multiply to obtain $$\begin{aligned} (8x^2-9x)(x-1)(x+1) &= (x+2)(3x+1)x(x-1) \\ 8x^4-9x^3-8x^2+9x &= 3x^4+x^3+2x^2-x+6x^3+2x \\ 5x^4+7x^3-6x^2-10x&=0 \\ x(5x^3+7x^2-6x-10) &= 0
\end{aligned} $$
Finally, we can use the Rational Root Theorem (or synthetic division) to factor the cubic polynomial, $5x^3+7x^2-6x-10$, and see if there are any rational roots. We find that $x=2$ is a root, so we can factor the polynomial as \begin{align*}
5x^3+7x^2-6x-10 &= (x-2)(5x^2+17x+5) \\
&= (x-2)\left[\left(x+\frac{-17+\sqrt{209}}{10}\right)\left(x+\frac{-17-\sqrt{209}}{10}\right)\right].
\end{align*} Therefore, the solutions to the original equation are $x=0$, $x=2$, and the two irrational roots of the quadratic. Writing the solutions in order from smallest to largest, we have $$\boxed{-2.246, -0.358, 0, 2.}$$

Sure, let me explain how to find the radical conjugate of $5 - 6\sqrt{2}$.

The radical conjugate of a number in the form $a + b\sqrt{c}$ is simply $a - b\sqrt{c}$. So in this case, the radical conjugate of $5 - 6\sqrt{2}$ would be $5 + 6\sqrt{2}$.

To see why this is the case, consider multiplying the original number by its radical conjugate:

$$(5 - 6\sqrt{2})(5 + 6\sqrt{2}) = 5^2 - (6\sqrt{2})^2 = 25 - 72 = -47$$

Notice that the product of the two numbers has no square root terms, which means it is a rational number. In particular, it is $-47$. 

So the radical conjugate of a number is useful when we want to get rid of square roots in the denominator of a fraction. We multiply the numerator and denominator by the radical conjugate so that the denominator becomes a rational number.

I hope that helps!

Let $x = \log_a b,$ $y = \log_b c,$ and $z = \log_c a.$  Then $x + y + z = 0,$ so
\[x^3 + y^3 + z^3 - 3xyz = (x + y + z)(x^2 + y^2 + z^2 - xy - xz - yz) = 0.\]Hence,
\[x^3 + y^3 + z^3 = 3xyz = 3 (\log_a b)(\log_b c)(\log_c a) = 3 \cdot \frac{\log b}{\log a} \cdot \frac{\log c}{\log b} \cdot \frac{\log a}{\log c} = \boxed{3}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\log_a b + \log_b c + \log_c a = 0,$
\begin{align*}
\log_a b + \log_b c &= -\log_c a, \\
\log_b c + \log_c a &= -\log_a b, \\
\log_c a + \log_a b &= -\log_b c.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[(\log_a b)^2 + (\log_b c)^2 + (\log_c a)^2 = (\log_a b + \log_b c)^2 + (\log_b c + \log_c a)^2 + (\log_c a + \log_a b)^2 = (\log_a b)^2 + (\log_b c)^2 + (\log_c a)^2.\]Then $(\log_a b)^2 + (\log_b c)^2 + (\log_c a)^2 = 0,$ so $\log_a b = \log_b c = \log_c a = 0.$  Hence, $a = b = c,$ so $(\log_a b)^3 + (\log_b c)^3 + (\log_c a)^3 = 3 \cdot 0 = \boxed{0}.$

Let $P = 2r + r \theta,$ the perimeter of the circular sector.  By AM-GM,
\[P = 2r + r \theta \ge 2 \sqrt{(2r)(r \theta)} = 2 \sqrt{2r^2 \theta}.\]Then $P^2 \ge 8r^2 \theta,$ so
\[\frac{r^2 \theta}{2} \le \frac{P^2}{16}.\]Equality occurs when $2r = r \theta,$ or $\theta = \boxed{2}.$

Yes, let me walk you through the problem!

The perimeter of the circular sector is fixed, which means that $r$ and $\theta$ are related.  From the picture, we see that the arc length of the sector is $r\theta.$  The remaining two sides of the sector each have length $r.$  Thus, the perimeter is
$$r + r + r\theta = 2r + r\theta.$$Since the perimeter is fixed, $2r + r\theta$ is also fixed, so $r$ and $\theta$ are not independent.  Solving for $r,$ we find
$$r = \frac{P}{2 + \theta}.$$Then, the area of the circular sector is
\[A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{P^2}{(2 + \theta)^2} \cdot \theta.\]We can maximize this by differentiating with respect to $\theta.$  We get
$$\frac{dA}{d\theta} = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{P^2}{(2 + \theta)^2} - \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2P^2}{(2 + \theta)^3} \cdot \theta.$$Setting the expression equal to 0 to find the maximum, we get
$$\frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{P^2}{(2 + \theta)^2} - \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2P^2}{(2 + \theta)^3} \cdot \theta = 0.$$This simplifies to
$$\frac{1}{2 + \theta} = \frac{\theta}{2}.$$Cross-multiplying gives $2\theta + \theta^2 = 4 + 2\theta,$ so $\theta^2 = 4,$ which means $\theta = \boxed{2}.$  (We reject $\theta = -2.$)

Sure, let's solve the equation:

\begin{align*}
\frac{2x + 3}{x - 4} - \frac{2x - 8}{2x + 1} &= 1 \\
\frac{(2x + 3)(2x + 1)}{(x - 4)(2x + 1)} - \frac{(2x - 8)(x - 4)}{(x - 4)(2x + 1)} &= 1 &&\quad\text{(finding a common denominator)} \\
\frac{(2x + 3)(2x + 1) - (2x - 8)(x - 4)}{(x - 4)(2x + 1)} &= 1 \\
\frac{4x^2 + 8x + 3 - (2x^2 - 12x + 32)}{(x - 4)(2x + 1)} &= 1 &&\quad\text{(expanding out the brackets)} \\
\frac{2x^2 + 20x - 29}{(x - 4)(2x + 1)} &= 1 \\
2x^2 + 20x - 29 &= x - 4 &&\quad\text{(multiplying through by the denominators)} \\
2x^2 + 19x - 25 &= 0.
\end{align*}

We can now solve for $x$ using the quadratic formula:

\begin{align*}
x &= \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \\
&= \frac{-19 \pm \sqrt{19^2 - 4 \cdot 2 \cdot (-25)}}{4} \\
&= \frac{-19 \pm \sqrt{777}}{4}.
\end{align*}

So the solutions are

$$x = \frac{-19 + \sqrt{777}}{4} \quad\text{and}\quad x = \frac{-19 - \sqrt{777}}{4}.$$

These are exact values, but if you need decimal approximations, you can use a calculator.

Extracting Answer from: 
First, we claim that any quartic with real coefficients can be written as the product of two quadratic polynomials with real coefficients.

Let $z$ be a complex root of the quartic.  If $z$ is not real, then its complex conjugate $\overline{z}$ is also a root.  Then the quadratic $(x - z)(x - \overline{z})$ has real coefficients, and when we factor out this quadratic, we are left with a quadratic that also has real coefficients.

If $z$ is real, then we can factor out $x - z,$ leaving us with a cubic with real coefficients.  Every cubic with real coefficients has at least one real roots, say $w.$  Then we can factor out $x - w,$ leaving us with a quadratic with real coefficients.  The product of this quadratic and $(x - z)(x - w)$ is the original quartic.

So, let
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 = (x^2 + px + r) \left( x^2 + qx + \frac{1}{r} \right), \quad (*)\]where $p,$ $q,$ and $r$ are real.

Suppose one quadratic factor has distinct real roots, say $z$ and $w.$  Then the only way that the quartic can be nonnegative for all real numbers $x$ is if the roots of the other quadratic are also $z$ and $w.$  Thus, we can write the quadratic as
\[(x - z)^2 (x - w)^2.\]Thus, we can assume that for each quadratic factor, the quadratic does not have real, distinct roots.  This implies that the discriminant of each quadratic is at most 0.  Thus,
\[p^2 \le 4r \quad \text{and} \quad q^2 \le \frac{4}{r}.\]It follows that $r > 0.$  Multiplying these inequalities, we get
\[p^2 q^2 \le 16,\]so $|pq| \le 4.$

Expanding $(*)$ and matching coefficients, we get
\begin{align*}
p + q &= a, \\
pq + r + \frac{1}{r} &= 3, \\
\frac{p}{r} + qr &= b.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
a^2 + b^2 &= (p + q)^2 + \left( \frac{p}{r} + qr \right)^2 \\
&= p^2 + 2pq + q^2 + \frac{p^2}{r^2} + 2pq + q^2 r^2 \\
&= p^2 + 4pq + q^2 + \frac{p^2}{r^2} + q^2 r^2 \\
&\le 4r + 4pq + \frac{4}{r} + \frac{4r}{r^2} + \frac{4}{r} \cdot r^2 \\
&= 4pq + 8r + \frac{8}{r}.
\end{align*}From the equation $pq + r + \frac{1}{r} = 3,$
\[r + \frac{1}{r} = 3 - pq,\]so
\[a^2 + b^2 \le 4pq + 8(3 - pq) = 24 - 4pq \le 40.\]To obtain equality, we must have $pq = -4$ and $r + \frac{1}{r} = 7.$  This leads to $r^2 - 7r + 1 = 0,$ whose roots are real and positive.  For either root $r,$ we can set $p = \sqrt{4r}$ and $q = -\sqrt{\frac{4}{r}},$ which shows that equality is possible.  For example, we can obtain the quartic
\[\left( x - \frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} \right)^2 \left( x + \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2} \right)^2 = x^4 - 2x^3 \sqrt{5} + 3x^2 + 2x \sqrt{5} + 1.\]Hence, the maximum value of $a^2 + b^2$ is $\boxed{40}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
First, we complete the square in $x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1.$  Note that
\begin{align*}
x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 &= x^4 + ax^3 + 2x^2 + x^2 + bx + 1 \\
&= x^4 + ax^3 + 2x^2 + 2x \cdot \frac{b}{2} + \frac{b^2}{4} - \frac{b^2}{4} + 1 \\
&= \left( x^2 + \frac{a}{2} x + 1 \right)^2 + \frac{1}{4} (4 - b^2).
\end{align*}Thus, we want
\[\left( x^2 + \frac{a}{2} x + 1 \right)^2 + \frac{1}{4} (4 - b^2) \ge 0\]for all $x.$  Thus, $\frac{1}{4} (4 - b^2) \ge 0,$ so $|b| \le 2.$

We want to make the expression as large as possible.  If the expression takes on its minimum value of 0 for all $x,$ then we must have
\[x^2 + \frac{a}{2} x + 1 = 0\]for all $x.$  Then $a = -2$ and $b = 2$ (or $a = 2$ and $b = -2$).

If we assume that the expression takes on positive values, then the expression is equivalent to a sum of squares, namely
\[\left( x^2 + \frac{ax}{2} + \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{4 - b^2} \right)^2 + \left( \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{4 - b^2} \right)^2.\]Then $a^2 + b^2$ is maximized when $\frac{ax}{2} = \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{4 - b^2}.$  Squaring both sides, we get
\[\frac{a^2 x^2}{4} = \frac{1}{4} (4 - b^2).\]Hence,
\[a^2 + b^2 = a^2 + 4 - a^2 = 4.\]Therefore, the largest possible value of $a^2 + b^2$ is $\boxed{4}.$

If $a_n =an + b$ and $b_n = cn + d$ are two arithmetic sequences, then their term-wise product takes the form \[a_nb_n = (an+b)(cn+d) = An^2 + Bn + C,\]where $A, B, C$ are constants. Therefore, some sequence $x_n = An^2 + Bn + C$ has $x_0 = 1440,$ $x_1 = 1716,$ and $x_2 = 1848.$ Then we have the equations \[\begin{aligned} C &= 1440, \\ A+B+C&=1716,\\  4A+2B+C&=1848. \end{aligned}\]Subtracting $C=1440$ from the second and third equations, we have $A+B=276$ and $4A+2B=408,$ or $2A+B=204.$ Then \[A = (2A+B) - (A+B) = 204 - 276 = -72,\]and so $B = 276-A=348.$ We conclude that the given sequence has the general formula \[x_n = -72n^2 + 348n + 1440.\]Then the eighth term is \[\begin{aligned} x_7 &= -72 \cdot 7^2 + 348 \cdot 7 + 1440 = \boxed{348}. \end{aligned}\]

Sure! Let's call the first arithmetic sequence $a_n$ and the second arithmetic sequence $b_n$. 

We know that the first term of $a_n$ is 1440, and the second term is 1716. This means that the common difference of $a_n$ is $1716-1440 = 276$. So, we can write the $n$th term of $a_n$ as $1440 + 276(n-1)$. 

Similarly, we know that the first term of $b_n$ is 1 (since multiplying by 1 doesn't change anything), and the second term is 1716/1440 = 1.19. So the common difference of $b_n$ is $1.19-1 = 0.19$. We can write the $n$th term of $b_n$ as $1 + 0.19(n-1)$. 

Now we can find the eighth term of the overall sequence: 

$$a_8b_8 = (1440 + 276(7))(1 + 0.19(7)) = 2156.04$$

So the eighth term of the sequence is approximately 2156.04.

Sure, I'd be happy to help you with that problem! This is an example of an infinite series that procedes infinitely in the radical underneath the square root sign.
We can start by letting
\[a = \sqrt{\cfrac{x}{1 + \sqrt{\cfrac{x}{1 + \sqrt{\cfrac{x}{1 + \cdots}}}}}.\]
We notice that the infinite series in the denominator of the fraction on the right is equivalent to simply $a$, so we can write:
\[a = \sqrt{\frac{x}{1+a}}.\]
Squaring both sides of this equation, we get
\[a^2 = \frac{x}{1+a}.\]
Multiplying both sides by $1+a$ and simplifying, we get
\[a^3 + a^2 =x,\]
Since we know $a$ is equal to $8$, we can substitute that into our equation and solve for $x.$
\begin{align*}
8^3 + 8^2 &= x \\
512 + 64 &= x \\
576 &= x
\end{align*}
Therefore, the positive value of $x$ for which the equation is true is $\boxed{576}$.

Note that maximizing $x(1 - x)^5$ is equivalent to maximizing $5x(1 - x)^5.$  Then by AM-GM,
\[\frac{5x + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x)}{6} \ge \sqrt[6]{5x (1 - x)^5}.\]This simplifies to $\sqrt[6]{5x (1 - x)^5} \le \frac{5}{6}.$  (Note how the left hand-side simplifies to a constant, which is why we consider $5x(1 - x)^5.$)  Hence,
\[x (1 - x)^5 \le \frac{1}{5} \left( \frac{5}{6} \right)^6 = \frac{3125}{46656}.\]Equality occurs when $5x = 1 - x,$ or $x = \frac{1}{6},$ so the maximum value is $\boxed{\frac{3125}{46656}}.$

Because the problem asks for the sum of the lengths of the intervals, we can replace $x$ with $x-2010,$ and the answer will not change. Then we have the inequality $$\frac{1}{x+1}+\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{x-1}\ge1.$$Let $f(x)=\frac{1}{x+1}+\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{x-1}$. Note that $f(x)$ has three vertical asymptotes at $x=-1, 0, 1.$ Since the function $g(x) = 1/x$ is decreasing over every connected interval on which it is defined, the same is true of $f(x).$ That is, $f(x)$ is decreasing on each of the intervals $(-\infty, -1),$ $(-1, 0),$ $(0,1),$ and $(1, \infty).$

As $x$ approaches $\infty$ and as $x$ approaches $-\infty,$ we see that $f(x)$ approaches $0.$ And as $x$ approaches each of the vertical asymptotes $x=-1, 0, 1$ from the left, $f(x)$ approaches $-\infty,$ while as $x$ approaches each of the vertical asymptotes from the right, $f(x)$ approaches $\infty.$ This lets us sketch the graph of $f(x),$ as shown below:
[asy]
size(12cm);
void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1)
{
    draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow);
    draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow);
    label("$x$",(x1,0),E);
    label("$y$",(0,y1),N);
    for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i)
        draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1));
    for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i)
        draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i));
}
axes(-5.9,7,-4.6,5);
real f(real x) { return 1/(x+1) + 1/x + 1/(x-1); }
draw(graph(f,-5.5,-1.4),Arrows);
draw((-1,-4.5)--(-1,4.5),dotted);
draw(graph(f,-.84,-.23),Arrows);
draw(graph(f,.2,.82),Arrows);
draw((1,-4.5)--(1,4.5),dotted);
draw(graph(f,1.3,6.5),Arrows);
draw((-2,1)--(5,1),blue,Arrows);
dot((-.675,1)^^(.4608,1)^^(3.214,1),blue);
[/asy]
Therefore, the equation $f(x) = 1$ has three roots $p, q, r,$ where $p \in (-1, 0),$ $q \in (0, 1),$ and $r \in (1, \infty).$ In terms of these roots, the values of $x$ such that $f(x) \ge 1$ are \[(-1, p] \cup (0, q] \cup (1, r].\]The sum of the lengths of the three intervals above is $(p+1) + q + (r-1) = p+q+r,$ so we want to find the sum of the roots of $f(x) = 1.$

Multiplying the equation $f(x) = \frac1{x+1} + \frac1x + \frac1{x-1} = 1$ by $(x+1)x(x-1)$ and then rearranging, we get the cubic equation \[x^3 - 3x^2 - x + 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, the sum of the roots of this equation is $\boxed{3}.$

Sure, I can help you with that problem. 

First, we'll simplify the given expression:

$$\dfrac{1}{x-2009}+\dfrac{1}{x-2010}+\dfrac{1}{x-2011}\ge 1$$

$$\dfrac{(x-2010)(x-2011)+(x-2009)(x-2011)+(x-2009)(x-2010)}{(x-2009)(x-2010)(x-2011)}\ge 1$$

$$\dfrac{3x^2-6030x+6063058}{(x-2009)(x-2010)(x-2011)}\ge 1$$

$$3x^2-6030x+6063058\ge (x-2009)(x-2010)(x-2011)$$

We notice that there are three roots, $x=2009$, $x=2010$, and $x=2011$. We can then split the real line into four intervals: $(-\infty,2009)$, $(2009,2010)$, $(2010,2011)$, and $(2011,\infty)$.

We then check each interval, testing a point within each interval to see if it satisfies the inequality:

For $x\in(-\infty,2009)$:

$$\dfrac{1}{x-2009}+\dfrac{1}{x-2010}+\dfrac{1}{x-2011}< 1$$

Pick $x=2008$: 

$$\dfrac{1}{-1} + \dfrac{1}{-2} +\dfrac{1}{-3} = \frac{-6}{6} < 1$$

so this interval doesn't work.

For $x\in(2009,2010)$:

$$\dfrac{1}{x-2009}+\dfrac{1}{x-2010}+\dfrac{1}{x-2011}\ge 1$$

Pick $x=2009.9$:

$$\dfrac{1}{2009.9-2009}+\dfrac{1}{2009.9-2010}+\dfrac{1}{2009.9-2011} \approx 1.00015 > 1$$

so this interval works.

For $x\in(2010,2011)$:

$$\dfrac{1}{x-2009}+\dfrac{1}{x-2010}+\dfrac{1}{x-2011}\ge 1$$

Pick $x=2010.5$:

$$\dfrac{1}{2010.5-2009}+\dfrac{1}{2010.5-2010}+\dfrac{1}{2010.5-2011} \approx 0.998 > 1$$

so this interval doesn't work.

For $x\in(2011,\infty)$:

$$\dfrac{1}{x-2009}+\dfrac{1}{x-2010}+\dfrac{1}{x-2011}\ge 1$$

Pick $x=2011.1$:

$$\dfrac{1}{2011.1-2009}+\dfrac{1}{2011.1-2010}+\dfrac{1}{2011.1-2011} \approx 1.00015 > 1$$

so this interval works.

Therefore the set of real numbers for which the given inequality is true is $(2009,2010] \cup (2011,\infty)$, and the sum of the lengths of those intervals is:

$$(2010-2009)+(+\infty-2011)=2+\infty=\boxed{\infty}$$

I hope that helped!

To see that $7$ works, we can consider $q(x) = x^7$. Then as $x$ grows away from $0$, the $x^7$ terms in the function will dominate and the function will tend towards $\frac{2x^7}{x^7} = 2$. Thus the smallest possible degree of $q(x)$ is $\boxed{7}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! To determine the smallest degree possible for $q(x)$, we need to consider what happens to the rational function as $x$ gets very large (both positively and negatively). 

Here's what we know: if $q(x)$ has a higher degree than the numerator polynomial $2x^7-5x^4 + 3x^3 -x+5$, then the rational function approaches 0 as $x$ goes to infinity or negative infinity. This is because the denominator grows faster than the numerator (and so the ratio approaches 0). 

But if $q(x)$ has the same degree as the numerator, then the rational function has a slant asymptote (see \#23 on this page. 

So what we want is for $q(x)$ to have the same degree as the numerator, but for the quotient to approach a horizontal line (rather than a slanted line) for very large values of $x$. This can be achieved by dividing $q(x)$ by $x^7$ -- that is, by setting $q(x) = x^7 r(x)$ for some polynomial $r(x)$. Then the original rational function becomes 

$$ \frac{2x^7-5x^4 + 3x^3 -x+5}{x^7r(x)} = \frac{2-5/x^3+3/x^4-1/x^6+5/x^7}{r(x)} $$

As $x$ approaches infinity, all the terms with negative exponents approach zero, leaving us with the expression $2/r(x)$. This means that the rational function approaches a horizontal line if and only if $r(x)$ approaches infinity as $x$ goes to infinity. 

But we also want $q(x)$ to have the smallest possible degree, so we should set $r(x)$ to be a constant (since any non-constant polynomial will approach infinity faster than a constant). Taking $r(x)=1$, we get $q(x)=x^7$, and the rational function has a horizontal asymptote. So the smallest possible degree for $q(x)$ is $\boxed{7}$.

By the Integer Root Theorem, any integer root must be a divisor of the constant term -- thus, in this case, a (positive or negative) divisor of $72$. However, this leaves quite a lot of candidates:
$$\pm 1,\ \pm 2,\ \pm 3,\ \pm 4,\ \pm 6,\ \pm 8,\ \pm 9,\ \pm 12,\ \pm 18,\ \pm 24,\ \pm 36,\ \pm 72.$$To narrow our choices, we define another polynomial.  Note that $g(1) = 77.$  Then by the Factor Theorem, $g(x) - 77$ is divisible by $x - 1.$  In other words,
$$g(x) = (x-1)q(x) + 77$$for some polynomial $q(x)$. Thus if we define $h(x) = g(x+1)$, then we have
$$h(x) = xq(x+1) + 77,$$so $h(x)$ has a constant term of $77$. Thus any integer root of $h(x)$ is a divisor of $77$; the possibilities are
$$-77,\ -11,\ -7,\ -1,\ 1,\ 7,\ 11,\ 77.$$This is useful because, if $x$ is a root of $g(x)$, then $h(x-1)=g(x)=0$, so $x-1$ must appear in the list of roots of $h(x)$. In particular, $x$ must be $1$ more than a root of $h(x)$, which gives the possibilities
$$-76,\ -10,\ -6,\ 0,\ 2,\ 8,\ 12,\ 78.$$Of these, only $-6$, $2$, $8$, and $12$ were candidates in our original list. Testing them one by one, we find that $x=\boxed{12}$ is the only integer root of $g(x)$.

We can write the function as
\[f(x) = \sqrt{(7 - x)(3 + x)} - \sqrt{(5 - x)(2 + x)}.\]This shows that the function is defined only for $-2 \le x \le 5.$  Also, $(7 - x)(3 + x) - (5 - x)(2 + x) = x + 11 > 0$ on this interval, which means that $f(x)$ is always positive.

Then
\begin{align*}
[f(x)]^2 &= (7 - x)(3 + x) - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(3 + x)} \sqrt{(5 - x)(2 + x)} + (5 - x)(2 + x) \\
&= -2x^2 + 7x + 31 - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)(5 - x)(3 + x)} \\
&= 2 + (7 - x)(2 + x) - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)} \sqrt{(5 - x)(3 + x)} + (5 - x)(3 + x) \\
&= 2 + \left[ \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)} - \sqrt{(5 - x)(3 + x)} \right]^2 \ge 2.
\end{align*}Therefore, $f(x) \ge \sqrt{2}.$

Equality occurs when $(7 - x)(2 + x) = (5 - x)(3 + x),$ or $x = \frac{1}{3}.$  We conclude that the minimum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{2}}.$

Sure! Let's first find the domain of the function. Inside the square roots, we have the expression $-x^2 + 4x + 21.$ We can write this as $-((x - 2)^2 - 5),$ which means the maximum value of $-x^2 + 4x + 21$ is $-5,$ which occurs when $x = 2.$ Similarly, we can write the expression $-x^2 + 3x + 10$ as $-((x - \tfrac{3}{2})^2 - \tfrac{1}{4}).$ This means the maximum value of $-x^2 + 3x + 10$ is $\tfrac{1}{4},$ which occurs when $x = \tfrac{3}{2}.$ Since we can take the square root of any nonnegative real number, we must have
\[x^2 - 4x - 21 \le 0 \quad \text{and} \quad x^2 - 3x - 10 \le 0.\]The first inequality factors as $(x - 7)(x + 3) \le 0,$ which means $-3 \le x \le 7.$ The second inequality factors as $(x - 5)(x + 2) \le 0,$ so $-2 \le x \le 5.$ Therefore, the domain of $f(x)$ is $[-2,5].$

Next, consider the function $g(x) = -x^2 + 4x + 21.$ The graph of this function is a parabola that opens downward, so its maximum value occurs at its vertex. We can complete the square to find the coordinates of the vertex:
\[g(x) = -(x - 2)^2 + 25.\]Thus, the vertex of $g(x)$ is $(2,25).$ Similarly, we can consider the function $h(x) = -x^2 + 3x + 10$ and use completing the square to find its vertex: $h(x) = -(x - \tfrac{3}{2})^2 + \tfrac{17}{4},$ so the vertex of $h(x)$ is $(\tfrac{3}{2},\tfrac{17}{4}).$

\[f(x) = \sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} - \sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} = \left\{
\begin{array}{ll}
\sqrt{(x - 2)^2 - 5} - \sqrt{(x - \tfrac{3}{2})^2 - \tfrac{1}{4}} & \text{if } -2 \le x \le \tfrac{3}{2}, \\
\sqrt{(x - 2)^2 - 5} - \sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} & \text{if } \tfrac{3}{2} \le x \le 5.
\end{array}
\right.\]Using the distances between vertices and intersections with the $x-$axis, we can sketch reasonable graph indicating that minimum value of $f(x)$ occurs at $x= \tfrac{7}{6}$, which evaluates to $f(\tfrac{7}{6})=\tfrac{\sqrt{29}}{2}-\tfrac{\sqrt{41}}{2}$.

Therefore, the minimum value of the function is $\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{29}}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{41}}{2}}.$

It's certainly possible to just calculate the complex number $2\omega^2-4\omega-30$ by just plugging in the value of $\omega$, but it's computationally simpler to use the fact that $|ab|=|a||b|$ and our knowledge of factoring quadratics: \begin{align*}
|2\omega^2-4\omega-30|&=|2(\omega-5)(\omega+3)|\\
&=2|\omega-5|\cdot|\omega+3|\\
&=2|-4-5i|\cdot|4-5i|\\
&=2\sqrt{(-4)^2+5^2}\sqrt{4^2+5^2}\\
&=\boxed{82}
\end{align*}

Certainly! Let's begin by simply plugging in the expression for $\omega$ into the given expression:

$$|2\omega^2-4\omega-30| = |2(1-5i)^2 - 4(1-5i) - 30|$$

Now we evaluate each term:

\begin{align*}
(1-5i)^2 &= 1 - 10i + 25i^2 = 1 - 10i - 25 = -24 - 10i \\
2(1-5i)^2 &= 2(-24 - 10i) = -48 - 20i \\
4(1-5i) &= 4 - 20i \\
-30 &= -30 \\
2\omega^2-4\omega-30 &= (-48-20i)-(4-20i)-30 \\
&= -48 - 4 - 30 - 40i \\
&= -82 - 40i
\end{align*}

So, we end up with:

$$|2\omega^2-4\omega-30| = |-82-40i|$$

To evaluate this absolute value, we can use the modulus formula:

$$|a+bi| = \sqrt{a^2 + b^2}$$

So in this case:

$$|{-82-40i}| = \sqrt{(-82)^2 + (-40)^2} = \sqrt{9808} \approx 99.04$$

Therefore, $|2\omega^2-4\omega-30| \approx 99.04$.

\[p(x) = c_{2005} x^{2005} + c_{2004} x^{2004} + \dots + c_1 x.\]If we multiply the $n$th equation by $c_n,$ then we get
\[
\begin{array}{ccccccccccc}
a_1 \cdot c_1 \cdot 1 & + & a_2 \cdot c_1 \cdot 2 & + & a_3 \cdot c_1 \cdot 3 & + & \dotsb & + & a_{2005} \cdot c_1 \cdot 2005 & = & 0, \\
a_1 \cdot c_2 \cdot 1^2 & + & a_2 \cdot c_2 \cdot 2^2 & + & a_3 \cdot c_2 \cdot 3^2 & + & \dotsb & + & a_{2005} \cdot c_2 \cdot 2005^2 & = & 0, \\

\end{array}
\]Note that the terms in the $k$th column add up to $p(k).$  Thus,
\[a_1 p(1) + a_2 p(2) + a_3 p(3) + \dots + a_{2005} p(2005) = c_{2005}.\]Note that this holds for any constants $c_1,$ $c_2,$ $\dots,$ $c_{2005}$ we choose. \[p(x) = x(x - 2)(x - 3) \dotsm (x - 2005).\]Then $p(1) = 2004!$ and $p(k) = 0$ for $k = 2,$, 3, $\dots,$ 2005, so
\[2004! \cdot a_1 = 1.\]Hence, $a_1 = \boxed{\frac{1}{2004!}}.$

Certainly! This problem seems pretty difficult, but it can actually be solved with some clever observation about the properties of finite differences! First, note that if we define $f(x) = a_1x + a_2x^2 + \cdots + a_{2005}x^{2005}$, then the given system of equations can be represented as $$\begin{array}{rcl} f(1) &=& 0 \\ f(2) &=& 0 \\ f(3) &=& 0 \\ \vdots && \\ f(2005) &=& 0 \\ f^{(2005)}(1) &=& 1,\end{array}$$ where $f^{(n)}(x)$ denotes the $n$th finite difference of $f(x)$ evaluated at $x$. This is because $a_n$ is simply the coefficient of $x^n$ in $f(x)$!

Now, the key idea is that we can use finite differences to find a polynomial $g(x)$ that has the same values as $f(x)$ for $x = 1, 2, \ldots, 2005$, but has an $a_1$ term which is easy to compute. Specifically, if we let $g(x) = \frac{1}{2005!}\Delta^{2005} f(x)$, where $\Delta$ is the forward difference operator, then it can be shown that $g(x)$ has the same values as $f(x)$ for $x = 1, 2, \ldots, 2005$. Furthermore, the coefficient of $x$ in $g(x)$ is simply $\Delta f(1) = f(2) - f(1) = 0$, so $g(x)$ has the form $$g(x) = a_1(x - 1)(x - 2)\cdots(x - 2005),$$ for some constant $a_1$. (We can expand the right-hand side using the factorial notation as $a_1 \cdot \frac{(x - 1)!}{(2004)!} \cdot \frac{(x - 2)!}{(2003)!} \cdots \frac{(x - 2005)!}{0!}$, which makes it clear that the coefficient of $x$ is zero.)

Now, note that since $f^{(2005)}(1) \neq 0$, we have $\Delta^{2004} f(1) \neq 0$, so $g^{(2005 - 1)}(1) = \frac{1}{2005!} f^{(2005)}(1) \neq 0$. Therefore, $g(x)$ has a linear factor at $x = 1$, which means that $a_1 = \boxed{0}$.

The inequality $\frac{ab + 1}{a + b} < \frac{3}{2}$ turn into
\[ab + 1 < \frac{3}{2} a + \frac{3}{2} b.\]Then
\[ab - \frac{3}{2} a - \frac{3}{2} b + 1 < 0.\]Applying Simon's Favorite Factoring Trick, we get
\[\left( a - \frac{3}{2} \right) \left( b - \frac{3}{2} \right) < \frac{5}{4}.\]Hence,
\[(2a - 3)(2b - 3) < 5.\]If $a = 1,$ then the inequality becomes
\[3 - 2b < 5,\]which is satisfied for any positive integer $b.$  Similarly, if $b = 1,$ then the inequality is satisfied for any positive integer $a.$

Otherwise, $a \ge 2$ and $b \ge 2,$ so $2a - 3 \ge 1$ and $2b - 3 \ge 1.$  Note that both $2a - 3$ and $2b - 3$ are odd, so $(2a - 3)(2b - 3)$ is odd, so their product can only be 1 or 3.  This leads us to the solutions $(a,b) = (2,2),$ $(2,3),$ and $(3,2).$

If $a = 1,$ then
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{b^3 + 1}{1 + b^3} = 1.\]Similarly, if $b = 1,$ then the expression also simplifies to 1.

For $(a,b) = (2,2),$
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{2^3 \cdot 2^3 + 1}{2^3 + 2^3} = \frac{65}{16}.\]For $(a,b) = (2,3)$ or $(3,2),$
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{2^3 \cdot 3^3 + 1}{2^3 + 3^3} = \frac{31}{5}.\]Hence, the largest possible value of the expression is $\boxed{\frac{31}{5}}.$

Sure, let's tackle that problem! 

First, let's try to simplify the given inequality: 

\begin{align*}
\frac{ab+1}{a+b} < \frac{3}{2} &\Rightarrow 2(ab+1) < 3(a+b) \\
&\Rightarrow 2ab+2 < 3a+3b \\
&\Rightarrow 2ab - 3a - 3b + 2 < 0 \\
&\Rightarrow (2a-3)(2b-3) < 7.
\end{align*}

Now we can use the fact that $a$ and $b$ are positive integers to try out different values of $(a,b)$ that satisfy the inequality $(2a-3)(2b-3) < 7.$ The possible pairs $(a,b)$ are: 

$$(2,2), (2,3), (2,4), (3,2), (3,3), (4,2), (5,2).$$ 

We can check that only the pairs $(a,b)=$ $(2,4)$ and $(4,2)$ actually satisfy the inequality. Now we can calculate the value $\frac{a^3b^3+1}{a^3+b^3}$ for each of these pairs and pick the maximum: 

\begin{align*}
\frac{a^3b^3+1}{a^3+b^3} &= \frac{(2^3)(4^3)+1}{2^3+4^3} = \frac{129}{34}, \\
\frac{a^3b^3+1}{a^3+b^3} &= \frac{(4^3)(2^3)+1}{4^3+2^3} = \frac{129}{34}.
\end{align*}

So the maximum possible value of $\frac{a^3b^3+1}{a^3+b^3}$ is $\boxed{\frac{129}{34}}.$

Minimizing $\frac{x + y}{x - y}$ is equivalent to minimizing
\[\frac{x + y}{x - y} + 1 = \frac{2x}{x - y} = \frac{-2x}{y - x}.\]Note that $-2x$ and $y - x$ are always positive, so to minimize this expression, we take $y = 4,$ the largest possible value of $y.$

Then minimizing $\frac{x + 4}{x - 4}$ is equivalent to minimizing
\[\frac{x + 4}{x - 4} - 1 = \frac{8}{x - 4} = -\frac{8}{4 - x}.\]Note that $4 - x$ is always positive, so to minimize this expression, we take $x = -3.$  Hence, the minimum value is $\frac{-3 + 4}{-3 - 4} = \boxed{-\frac{1}{7}}.$

Sure! We can start by finding the range of values that $\frac{x+y}{x-y}$ can take on. If we call $z=\frac{x+y}{x-y}$, then we can solve for $y$ in terms of $x$ in the numerator of the expression: $$\frac{x+y}{x-y} = \frac{x+y}{x-y} \cdot \frac{x+y}{x+y} = \frac{(x+y)^2}{x^2 - y^2} = \frac{(x+y)^2}{(x-y)(x+y)} = \frac{x+y}{x-y} \cdot \frac{x+y}{x+y} = \frac{(x+y)^2}{(x-y)^2}.$$ Solving for $z$, we get $$z = \frac{(x+y)^2}{(x-y)^2} \quad \Rightarrow \quad (x-y)^2 z = (x+y)^2.$$ Now we can use the given ranges for $x$ and $y$: $$\begin{aligned} -5 \leq x &\leq -3, \\ 2 \leq y &\leq 4. \end{aligned}$$ Substituting in these values, we get $$\begin{aligned} (-5)-4 &\leq x+y \leq (-3)+4 \quad \Rightarrow \quad -9 \leq x+y \leq 1, \\ (-5)-2 &\leq x-y \leq (-3)+2 \quad \Rightarrow \quad -7 \leq x-y \leq -1. \end{aligned}$$ Since the denominator $(x-y)$ must be negative in order for $z$ to be positive, we can restrict our attention to the lower bound for $x-y$: $x-y \geq -7$. Then we can solve for the minimum value of $z$: $$\begin{aligned} (x-y)^2 z &= (x+y)^2, \\ z &= \frac{(x+y)^2}{(x-y)^2}, \\ z &\geq \frac{(x+y)^2}{(-7)^2} = \frac{(x+y)^2}{49}. \end{aligned}$$ Since $x+y$ takes on values between $-9$ and $1$, the minimum possible value of $(x+y)^2$ is $(-9)^2 = 81$. Therefore, the minimum possible value of $z$ is $$z \geq \frac{(x+y)^2}{49} \geq \frac{81}{49} > 1.$$ So the smallest possible value of $\frac{x+y}{x-y}$ is $\boxed{\frac{81}{49}}$.

We can write
\[\frac{a^2 + b^2}{a - b} = \frac{a^2 + b^2 - 2ab + 16}{a - b} = \frac{(a - b)^2 + 16}{a - b} = a - b + \frac{16}{a - b}.\]By AM-GM,
\[a - b + \frac{16}{a - b} \ge 2 \sqrt{(a - b) \cdot \frac{16}{a - b}} = 8.\]Equality occurs when $a - b = 4$ and $ab = 8.$  We can solve these equations to find $a = 2 \sqrt{3} + 2$ and $b = 2 \sqrt{3} - 2.$  Thus, the minimum value is $\boxed{8}.$

Sure, let's solve it! 

We begin by factoring the numerator $a^2 + b^2 = (a - b)^2 + 2ab$. We can substitute $8$ for $ab$ to get $(a-b)^2 + (2)(8)$. Now, we focus on the denominator $a-b$ which can be rewritten as $\frac{a}{b} - 1$. We know that $ab=8$ so that $\frac{a}{b} = \frac{8}{b^2}$.

Putting together the factored numerator and rewritten denominator, we have:

$$\frac{a^2 + b^2}{a - b} = \frac{(a-b)^2 + (2)(8)}{\frac{a}{b} - 1}= \frac{(a-b)^2 + 16}{\frac{8}{b^2} - 1} = \frac{(a-b)^2 + 16}{\frac{8-b^2}{b^2}} = \frac{(a-b)^2b^2 + 16b^2}{8-b^2} $$

Note that the function is undefined at $b=0$ so that our minimum value must be greater than $0$. Let's now take the derivative of this function with respect to $b$:

$$\frac{d}{db} \left(\frac{(a-b)^2b^2 + 16b^2}{8-b^2}\right) = \frac{4(3b^4-48)}{(b^2-8)^2}$$

This derivative is zero only at $b=\sqrt[4]{16}$ and $b=-\sqrt[4]{16}$. Since $a>b>0$, we only consider the positive value $b=\sqrt[4]{16} = 2$ to get:

$$\frac{a^2 + b^2}{a - b} \ge \frac{(a-2)^2 \cdot 4 + 16 \cdot 4 }{8 - 4} = 8a+12$$

Therefore, the minimum value of $\frac{a^2+b^2}{a-b}$ is $8a+12$, which can be made arbitrarily small by making $a$ large enough.

I hope this helps! Let me know if you have any questions or if there's anything else I can help you with.

Since $f$ has rational coefficients, $1-2\sqrt{3}$ and $3+\sqrt{2}$ must also be roots of $f(x).$ Therefore, $f$ must be divisible by the two polynomials \[(x-(1+2\sqrt3))(x-(1-2\sqrt3)) = x^2 - 2x - 11\]and \[(x-(3-\sqrt2))(x-(3+\sqrt2))=x^2-6x+7,\]so we have \[f(x) = A(x^2-2x-11)(x^2-6x+7)\]for some constant $A.$ Setting $x=0,$ we get \[f(0)=A(-11)(7) = -77A,\]so $-77A = -154,$ and $A=2.$ Thus, \[f(x) = 2(x^2-2x-11)(x^2-6x+7)\]and so $f(1) = 2(-12)(2) = \boxed{-48}.$

Certainly $f(x)$ has $1-2\sqrt{3}$ and $3+\sqrt{2}$ as its other roots, which are both irrational because the coefficients are rational. Thus, \[f(x) = a(x-(1+2\sqrt{3}))(x-(1-2\sqrt{3}))(x-(3+\sqrt{2}))(x-(3-\sqrt{2}))\] for some rational number $a.$ Expanding out the right-hand side, we have \[\begin{aligned} a(x^2 - 2x(1+2\sqrt{3}) + (1-2\sqrt{3})(1+2\sqrt{3}))(x^2 - 2x(3+\sqrt{2}) + (3+\sqrt{2})(3-\sqrt{2})) &= a(x^2 - 2x(1+2\sqrt{3}) - 23)(x^2 - 2x(3+\sqrt{2}) - 1) \\ &= a((x^2 - 2x(1+2\sqrt{3}) + 9) - 32)((x^2 - 2x(3+\sqrt{2}) + 2) - 3) \\ &= a((x-(1+2\sqrt{3}))^2 - 32)((x-(3+\sqrt{2}))^2 - 3). \end{aligned}\]So, $(x-(1+2\sqrt{3}))^2 - 32$ and $(x-(3+\sqrt{2}))^2 - 3$ are both factors of $f(x),$ and it follows that \[f(x) = a((x-(1+2\sqrt{3}))(x-(1-2\sqrt{3}) - 32)((x-(3+\sqrt{2})(x-(3-\sqrt{2}) - 3)).\]Note that $\sqrt{3} > 1,$ so $1 - 2\sqrt{3} < 0$ in the first factor, implying that \[(x-(1+2\sqrt{3}))(x-(1-2\sqrt{3})) = x^2 - 2x - 23\]is itself irreducible over the rationals. Similarly, the fact that $\sqrt{2} < 2$ yields \[(x-(3+\sqrt{2}))(x-(3-\sqrt{2})) = x^2 - 6x + 7\]as the other quadratic factor with rational coefficients. Thus, $f(x) = a(x^2-2x-23)(x^2-6x+7),$ so \[f(0) = a(-23)(7) = -161a = -154,\]which gives $a = \frac{22}{23}.$ Therefore, \[f(1) = \frac{22}{23}(1^2 - 2\cdot 1 - 23)(1^2 - 6\cdot 1 + 7) = \boxed{-\frac{1200}{529}}.\]

Dividing by $36$ gives the standard form of the equation of this ellipse, \[\frac{x^2}{4} + \frac{y^2}{36} = 1.\]Then, the semimajor and semiminor axes have lengths $\sqrt{36} = 6$ and $\sqrt{4} = 2,$ respectively. Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! We can solve this using the formula for the area of an ellipse, which is 

$$A = \pi ab,$$
where $a$ and $b$ are the lengths of the two perpendicular axes of the ellipse. 

To find $a$ and $b$ for the given equation, we want to arrange the equation so that it's in standard form, which has the form
$$\frac{(x-h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y-k)^2}{b^2} = 1,$$
where $(h,k)$ is the center of the ellipse. 

We first divide both sides of the equation $9x^2 + y^2 = 36$ by 36 to get 

$$\frac{x^2}{4} + \frac{y^2}{36} = 1.$$

Comparing this with the standard form, we see that $a=2$ and $b=6$. Thus, the area of the ellipse is 

$$A = \pi(2)(6) = \boxed{12\pi}.$$ 

I hope that helps! Let me know if you have any other questions.

Sure! Let's start by multiplying both sides by the denominator on the right side of the equation, $x+1$, to get:

$$(2x^2 + x + 3)(x + 1) = (2x + 1)(x^2 + x + 1)$$

Expanding both sides, we get:

$$2x^3 + 4x^2 + 2x + 3x^2 + 4x + 3 = 2x^3 + 2x^2 + 2x + x^2 + x + 1$$

Simplifying the left side by combining like terms, we get:

$$2x^3 + 7x^2 + 6x + 3 = 2x^3 + 3x^2 + 3x + 1$$

Subtracting $2x^3$ from both sides, we get:

$$7x^2 + 6x + 3 = 3x^2 + 3x + 1$$

Subtracting $3x^2 + 3x + 1$ from both sides, we get:

$$4x^2 + 3 = 0$$

Subtracting $3$ from both sides, we get:

$$4x^2 = -3$$

Finally, dividing both sides by $4$, we get:

$$x^2 = -\frac{3}{4}$$

Since $x^2$ can't be negative, there is no real solution for $x$.

From $a + 3b + 4c = 25,$ $a^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 25,$ we get
\begin{align*}
(3b + 4c)^2 &= 9b^2 + 24bc + 16c^2 \\
&= 25 - a^2 - c^2 \\
&= a.
\end{align*}By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(3^2 + 4^2)(b^2 + c^2) \ge (3b + 4c)^2,\]so $25 - a^2 \ge \frac{25a^2}{25} = a^2,$ or $0 \ge a^2 - 25a.$  Thus, $a \le 0$ or $a \ge 25.$

If $a \le 0,$ then $a^2 - 25a \ge 0,$ so $25 - a^2 + (a^2 - 25a) \le 25,$ which means $b^2 + c^2 \le 0.$  But $b^2$ and $c^2$ are nonnegative, so $b^2 + c^2 = 0.$  Hence, $b = 0$ and $c = 0,$ so $a = 25.$

If $a \ge 25,$ then $a^2 - 25a \ge 0,$ so $25 - a^2 + (a^2 - 25a) \le 25 + 2(a - 25) \le 25,$ which means $b^2 + c^2 \le 0.$  But $b^2$ and $c^2$ are nonnegative, so $b^2 + c^2 = 0.$  Hence, $b = 0$ and $c = 0,$ so $a = 25.$

Therefore, the largest possible value of $a$ is $\boxed{25}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Hello! As your chatbot tutor, I'd be happy to help you with this problem from your precalculus class.

First, let's understand what the problem is asking. We are given a certain integer $k$, which is equal to the greatest integer less than or equal to the cube root of each of the 70 positive integers $n_1, n_2, \ldots, n_{70}$, and where $k$ divides each of these numbers $n_i$. We are asked to find the maximum value of $\displaystyle\frac{n_i}{k}$ for $1 \leq i \leq 70$.

Let's start by looking at the relationship between $k$ and the $n_i$. Since $k$ is the greatest integer less than or equal to the cube root of each $n_i$, we know that $k^3 \leq n_i < (k+1)^3$. We are also told that $k$ divides each $n_i$, so we can write $n_i = k \cdot m_i$ for some integer $m_i$.

Substituting this expression for $n_i$ into the inequality, we get $k^3 \leq k \cdot m_i < (k+1)^3$. We can simplify this inequality by dividing both sides by $k$ (knowing that $k$ is a positive integer), giving $k^2 \leq m_i < (k+1)^2$. 

Thus, the value of $m_i$ lies in the range of consecutive integers between $k^2$ and $(k+1)^2 - 1$ (inclusive), and we know that $m_i$ is divisible by $k$. How many integers are in this range, and how many of them are divisible by $k$?

The number of integers in this range is $(k+1)^2 - k^2 = 2k+1$, and every other integer in this range is divisible by $k$. Therefore, there are $\frac{2k+1}{2} = k + \frac{1}{2}$ integers in this range that are divisible by $k$.

We have 70 values of $n_i$ that satisfy these conditions, so the sum of the values of $m_i$ is $k\cdot 70$, and the number of values of $m_i$ that are divisible by $k$ is $70\cdot (k + \frac{1}{2}) = 70k + 35$.

Now we can find the sum of the values of $m_i$. Since $n_i = k\cdot m_i$, we have $\displaystyle\sum_{i=1}^{70} n_i = k\cdot \sum_{i=1}^{70} m_i = k\cdot (70k) = 70k^2$.

We want to find the maximum value of $\displaystyle\frac{n_i}{k}$, which is equivalent to finding the maximum value of $m_i$. Since the values $m_i$ that satisfy the conditions lie within the range of $k^2$ to $(k+1)^2 - 1$, the maximum value of $m_i$ is $(k+1)^2 - 1$, and the maximum value of $\displaystyle\frac{n_i}{k}$ is $(k+1)^2 - 1$ (since $n_i = k\cdot m_i$).

Therefore, our problem reduces to finding the value of $k$ such that $(k+1)^2 - 1$ is maximized. Expanding $(k+1)^2 - 1$ gives us $k^2 + 2k$, so we want to maximize $k^2 + 2k$. We can do this by completing the square: $k^2 + 2k = (k+1)^2 - 1$.

Thus, the maximum value of $(k+1)^2 - 1$ (and therefore the maximum value of $\displaystyle\frac{n_i}{k}$) occurs when $k + 1 = -\frac{1}{2}$, or $k = -\frac{3}{2}$. However, we are given that $k$ is an integer, so we take the next lowest integer value of $k$, which is $k = -1$. 

Therefore, the maximum value of $\displaystyle\frac{n_i}{k}$ occurs when $k = -1$, which gives us $\displaystyle\frac{n_i}{k} = (k+1)^2 - 1 = (0)^2 - 1 = \boxed{-1}$.
